## SECTION 7: CONCEPTUAL APPARATUS

### 7.1 Key Terms and Their Definitions

1. **Weak Reductionism**: The text defines weak reductionism as the idea that, for any given truth in a higher-level domain, there is a corresponding truth in a lower-level domain that makes it non-anomalous.
   - Example: "Botany is reducible to microphysics in the weak sense that, for any given botanical truth, there is some microphysical truth with respect to which the former is non-anomalous."

2. **Strong Reductionism**: The text defines strong reductionism as the idea that the system of truths in a higher-level domain can be deduced from or understood in terms of the truths in a lower-level domain.
   - Example: "Botany is irreducible to microphysics in the sense that the system of botanical truths that botany puts forth, containing as it does botanical principles alongside the specific botanical truths it contains, cannot be deduced from, or otherwise understood in terms of, the truths of microphysics."

3. **Analytic Truths**: The text defines analytic truths as truths that delineate concepts but do not say anything about their instantiation.
   - Example: "So the truths of microphysics, taken together with truths describing the concepts of botany---truths that, since they merely delineate those concepts and don't say anything as to whether they're instantiated or by what, are analytic---entail the truths of botany."

4. **Psychological Truths**: The text defines psychological truths as truths that concern experiences rather than physical properties or changes.
   - Example: "Whatever information psychological truths do contain, that information is not to the effect that brains or physiological or otherwise sense-knowable entities have (undergone) some sense-knowable property (change). That information is about the experiences through which such changes are known, not the changes themselves."

### 7.2 Technical Vocabulary Introduced or Employed

1. **Microphysical Truths**: The text uses the term "microphysical truths" to refer to truths about the fundamental physical constituents of reality.
   - Example: "So if M*1....M*n is the least inclusive list of all of the microphysical truths describing the thing constituting Brown's brain..."

2. **Botanical Truths**: The text uses the term "botanical truths" to refer to truths about plants and their properties.
   - Example: "So the truths of microphysics, taken together with truths describing the concepts of botany---truths that, since they merely delineate those concepts and don't say anything as to whether they're instantiated or by what, are analytic---entail the truths of botany."

3. **Least Inclusive List**: The text uses the term "least inclusive list" to refer to the minimal set of truths necessary to describe a given domain.
   - Example: "So if M*1....M*n is the least inclusive list of all of the microphysical truths describing the thing constituting Brown's brain..."

### 7.3 Distinctions Drawn Between Related Concepts

1. **Weak vs. Strong Reductionism**: The text draws a distinction between weak and strong reductionism, arguing that while botany is weakly reducible to microphysics, it is strongly irreducible.
   - Example: "So there is a sense, albeit a weak one, in which botany is reducible to microphysics. Botany is reducible to microphysics in the weak sense that, for any given botanical truth, there is some microphysical truth with respect to which the former is non-anomalous. Botany is irreducible to microphysics in the sense that the system of botanical truths that botany puts forth, containing as it does botanical principles alongside the specific botanical truths it contains, cannot be deduced from, or otherwise understood in terms of, the truths of microphysics."

2. **Psychological vs. Physical Descriptions**: The text draws a distinction between psychological and physical descriptions, arguing that psychological truths concern experiences rather than physical properties or changes.
   - Example: "Whatever information psychological truths do contain, that information is not to the effect that brains or physiological or otherwise sense-knowable entities have (undergone) some sense-knowable property (change). That information is about the experiences through which such changes are known, not the changes themselves."

### 7.4 Theoretical Innovations or Neologisms

1. **Weak and Strong Irreducibility**: The text introduces the concepts of weak and strong irreducibility as theoretical innovations to discuss the nature of reductionism in different domains.
   - Example: "Mental truths are both weakly and strongly irreducible to physical truths."

### 7.5 Repurposing of Existing Concepts

1. **Reductionism**: The text repurposes the concept of reductionism, traditionally used in the context of scientific theories, to discuss the relationship between mental and physical truths.
   - Example: "Mental truths are both weakly and strongly irreducible to physical truths."

2. **Analytic Truths**: The text repurposes the concept of analytic truths, traditionally used in logic and philosophy of language, to discuss the role of conceptual truths in the reductionism debate.
   - Example: "So the truths of microphysics, taken together with truths describing the concepts of botany---truths that, since they merely delineate those concepts and don't say anything as to whether they're instantiated or by what, are analytic---entail the truths of botany."

## SECTION 8: ARGUMENTATIVE STRUCTURE

### 8.1 Overall Organization of the Argument

1. **Introduction of Weak Reductionism in Botany**: The text begins by discussing the weak reducibility of botany to microphysics, using the analogy of a building made of bricks to illustrate the concept.
   - This sets the stage for the discussion of reductionism and its limitations.

2. **Introduction of Strong Irreducibility in Botany**: The text then introduces the concept of strong irreducibility, arguing that botany cannot be fully reduced to microphysics despite the weak reducibility.
   - This prepares the reader for the more complex discussion of mental truths.

3. **Application to Psychology**: The text then applies the concepts of weak and strong reductionism to psychology, arguing that mental truths are weakly irreducible to physical truths.
   - This step builds on the previous discussion of botany and introduces the unique challenges of reducing mental states.

4. **Strong Irreducibility in Psychology**: The text concludes by arguing that mental truths are strongly irreducible to physical truths, using detailed examples and logical reasoning to support this claim.
   - This final step completes the argument and reinforces the thesis that mental truths cannot be fully reduced to physical truths.

### 8.2 Logical Dependencies Between Claims

1. **Weak Reductionism in Botany**: The argument for weak reductionism in botany is a foundational claim that sets the stage for the discussion of strong irreducibility.
   - The weak reducibility of botany to microphysics is necessary to understand the limitations of reductionism and the distinction between weak and strong forms.

2. **Strong Irreducibility in Botany**: The argument for strong irreducibility in botany depends on the prior establishment of weak reductionism.
   - The strong irreducibility of botany to microphysics is a more complex claim that builds on the understanding of weak reductionism and its limitations.

3. **Weak Irreducibility in Psychology**: The argument for weak irreducibility in psychology depends on the prior discussion of weak and strong reductionism in botany.
   - The analogy between botany and psychology is used to illustrate the concept of weak irreducibility and its application to mental truths.

4. **Strong Irreducibility in Psychology**: The argument for strong irreducibility in psychology depends on the prior establishment of weak irreducibility and the distinction between psychological and physical descriptions.
   - The strong irreducibility of mental truths to physical truths is the central claim of the text, building on the previous arguments and examples.

### 8.3 Progression from Premises to Conclusions

1. **Premise 1**: Botany is weakly reducible to microphysics because, for any given botanical truth, there is a corresponding microphysical truth that makes it non-anomalous.
2. **Premise 2**: Botany is strongly irreducible to microphysics because the system of botanical truths cannot be deduced from or understood in terms of microphysical truths alone.
3. **Premise 3**: Mental truths are weakly irreducible to physical truths because, even with complete knowledge of the microphysical events in a brain, one cannot infer any psychological truths about the person.
4. **Premise 4**: Mental truths are strongly irreducible to physical truths because the conjunction of microphysical truths and analytic psychological truths does not entail any psychological truths about a person.
5. **Conclusion**: Mental truths are both weakly and strongly irreducible to physical truths, highlighting the unique nature of mental states and their relationship to physical states.

### 8.4 Use of Examples, Illustrations, or Case Studies

1. **Building Made of Bricks**: The text uses the example of a building made of bricks to illustrate the concept of weak reductionism in botany.
   - Example: "Given a building composed entirely out of bricks, if one knew how any two of those bricks were spatiotemporally interrelated, one could in principle figure out the structure of that building."

2. **Smith and Brown's Brain**: The text uses the example of Smith trying to infer psychological truths from microphysical data about Brown's brain to illustrate the concept of weak and strong irreducibility in psychology.
   - Example: "By reasoning analogous to that just put forth, Smith could in principle infer everything there is to know about Brown's brain. But, given only that data, he could infer not one single psychological truth about Brown."

### 8.5 Anticipation and Response to Objections

1. **Objection to Psychological Indistinguishability**: The text anticipates the objection that psychologists care about the accuracy of experiences (e.g., hallucinations) and responds by arguing that this is only relevant because hallucinations are symptoms of psychological disturbances.
   - Example: "'But doesn't the psychologist care whether somebody is hallucinating or not?' Yes, but only because hallucinations are typically symptoms of psychological disturbances. The envatted brain's experiences are not thus symptomatic."

2. **Objection to Hempel's Views**: The text anticipates the objection that co-extensiveness between types in different domains is sufficient for reduction and responds by arguing that even if this were true, botany still couldn't be reduced to microphysics in a strong sense.
   - Example: "In fact, we have seen that, contrary to what Hempel (1965) assumed, even if we happened to live in a world in which botanical-types happened to be co-extensive with microphysical types, botany still couldn't be reduced, at least not in this robust sense, to microphysics."

### 8.6 Dialectical Structure

1. **Thesis**: Mental truths are both weakly and strongly irreducible to physical truths.
2. **Antithesis**: Reductionism, particularly the strong form, posits that higher-level truths can be fully reduced to lower-level truths.
3. **Synthesis**: The text synthesizes the thesis and antithesis by arguing that while there is a weak sense in which mental truths can be related to physical truths, they cannot be fully reduced to them in a strong sense.
   - This dialectical structure is evident in the detailed discussion of weak and strong reductionism and the refutation of opposing views.

## SECTION 1: DOMAIN & DISCIPLINARY CONTEXT

### 1.1 Primary Academic Domain(s) and Subdomain(s)

1. **Philosophy of Mind**: The text primarily falls within the domain of philosophy of mind, specifically addressing the relationship between psychological and physiological states.
   - **Subdomain**: Philosophy of Psychology, focusing on the nature of psychological truths and their relation to physiological truths.

2. **Philosophy of Science**: The text also engages with the philosophy of science, particularly in its discussion of the methodologies and epistemological foundations of psychology versus physical sciences.
   - **Subdomain**: Epistemology of Science, examining how knowledge is acquired and justified in different scientific fields.

### 1.2 Interdisciplinary Connections and Influences

1. **Cognitive Science**: The text's discussion of psycho-physical correlations and the nature of psychological knowledge intersects with cognitive science, which studies the mind and its processes.

2. **Neuroscience**: The mention of brain centers and neurological differences in mediating psychological abilities connects to neuroscience, which studies the nervous system and its role in behavior and cognition.

3. **Psychology**: The text's focus on psychological truths and the nature of psychological knowledge directly relates to psychology, particularly in its theoretical and epistemological aspects.

### 1.3 Historical Positioning Within the Field

1. **Mind-Body Problem**: The text engages with the long-standing philosophical debate over the mind-body problem, specifically addressing the relationship between mental states and physical states.
   - Historically, this debate has roots in Cartesian dualism and has evolved through various forms of materialism and dualism.

2. **Reductionism vs. Non-Reductionism**: The text positions itself within the debate over whether psychological phenomena can be reduced to physiological explanations, aligning more closely with non-reductionist views.

### 1.4 Relationship to Major Schools of Thought

1. **Non-Reductive Physicalism**: The text aligns with non-reductive physicalism, which posits that while mental states supervene on physical states, they cannot be fully explained or reduced to them.

2. **Functionalism**: The discussion of psychological types and their migration across different neurological structures resonates with functionalist theories of mind, which focus on the functional roles of mental states.

3. **Epistemological Internalism**: The emphasis on non-perceptual knowledge of mental states suggests a connection to epistemological internalism, which holds that knowledge is determined by factors internal to the subject.

### 1.5 Target Audience and Scholarly Community

1. **Philosophers of Mind and Psychology**: The primary audience includes philosophers specializing in the philosophy of mind and psychology, who are interested in the nature of psychological truths and their relation to physiological truths.

2. **Cognitive Scientists and Neuroscientists**: The text's discussion of psycho-physical correlations and neurological structures would also be of interest to cognitive scientists and neuroscientists.

3. **Philosophers of Science**: The text's engagement with the epistemology of science and the comparison between psychology and physical sciences would appeal to philosophers of science.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

1. **Primary Formulation**: Psychological truths cannot be deduced from or understood in terms of physiological truths, even if psychological types were co-extensive with physiological types.

2. **Secondary Formulation**: The knowledge of psychological states is fundamentally different from the knowledge of physical states, involving non-perceptual, direct knowledge that cannot be fully captured by physiological explanations.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Argument 1: Psycho-Physical Correlations**
   - **Explanation**: To acquire knowledge of psychological truths about a person, one must know psycho-physical correlations. This means that the best one can do in absorbing psychology into physiology is to establish correlations, not deductions or reductions.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text states, "Smith would have to know that when thus and such microphysical propositions (about the heap of particles constituting Brown's brain) held, then thus and such psychological truths (about Brown) also held."

2. **Argument 2: Non-Identity of Psychological and Physiological Types**
   - **Explanation**: Even if psychological types were co-extensive with physiological types, they are not necessarily co-extensive, meaning psychological truths reveal different facts about the world than physiological truths.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text uses the analogy of redness and roundness in a hypothetical world W, stating, "Even in a world W where all red objects were round, and no non-round objects were red, the information provided by the proposition x is red would be different from that provided by x is round."

3. **Argument 3: Migration of Psychological Abilities**
   - **Explanation**: Psychological abilities can migrate across different neurological structures, indicating that psychological types are not identical to physiological types.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text mentions, "A given ability on somebody's part; e.g., that person's ability to drive or speak English, can migrate. It often happens that, if brain-center A, which thus far has mediated Smith's ability to drive, is damaged and can no longer do its job, some other brain-center B takes over A's job---notwithstanding that A and B are neurologically very different."

4. **Argument 4: Non-Perceptual Knowledge of Psychological States**
   - **Explanation**: Psychological states are known in a non-perceptual way, which is fundamentally different from the sense-based knowledge of physical states.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text states, "The truths that it is the purpose of psychology to model are such that, unless one has had some kind of non-perceptual encounter with those things or with things of the same kind, one couldn't possibly understand them."

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **Subsidiary Claim 1: Knowledge by Acquaintance vs. Knowledge by Description**
   - **Explanation**: Knowledge of one's own mental states is by acquaintance, while knowledge of others' mental states is by description, yet both are informed by non-perceptual knowledge.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text mentions, "So even though I cannot possibly have the direct, intuitive knowledge of your mental states that you can have of them---even though I can do no better than to have 'knowledge by description' of them, whereas you can have knowledge 'by acquaintance' of them---my knowledge of your mental states isn't purely formal; it is informed by a knowledge of relevantly similar states in myself, my knowledge of which is completely non-perceptual."

2. **Subsidiary Claim 2: Anomaly of Psychology with Respect to Physical Sciences**
   - **Explanation**: Psychology is anomalous with respect to physical sciences because its subject matter involves non-perceptual knowledge that cannot be fully captured by the methodologies of physical sciences.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text states, "Why there is a sense in which psychology is anomalous with respect to the physical that is deeper than the sense a given physical discipline could possibly be anomalous with respect to another physical discipline."

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Assumption 1: Non-Reductive Nature of Psychological Truths**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes that psychological truths cannot be reduced to physiological truths, which is a foundational assumption for the non-reductive physicalist position.

2. **Assumption 2: Importance of Non-Perceptual Knowledge**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes that non-perceptual knowledge is crucial for understanding psychological states, which underpins the argument about the difference between psychological and physiological knowledge.

3. **Assumption 3: Functionalist View of Psychological Abilities**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes a functionalist perspective on psychological abilities, which allows for the migration of these abilities across different neurological structures.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

1. **Premise 1**: Psychological truths require knowledge of psycho-physical correlations.
2. **Premise 2**: Psychological types are not necessarily co-extensive with physiological types.
3. **Premise 3**: Psychological abilities can migrate across different neurological structures.
4. **Premise 4**: Psychological states are known in a non-perceptual way.
5. **Conclusion**: Psychological truths cannot be deduced from or understood in terms of physiological truths, and the knowledge of psychological states is fundamentally different from the knowledge of physical states.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

1. **Introduction of Psycho-Physical Correlations**: The text begins by establishing the necessity of psycho-physical correlations for understanding psychological truths, setting the stage for the non-reductive argument.

2. **Discussion of Co-Extensiveness**: The text then explores the concept of co-extensiveness between psychological and physiological types, using the analogy of redness and roundness to illustrate that even co-extensive types reveal different facts about the world.

3. **Migration of Psychological Abilities**: The text introduces the idea of psychological abilities migrating across different neurological structures, further supporting the non-identity of psychological and physiological types.

4. **Non-Perceptual Knowledge**: The text shifts to discussing the non-perceptual nature of psychological knowledge, contrasting it with the sense-based knowledge of physical states.

5. **Anomaly of Psychology**: Finally, the text concludes by highlighting the anomaly of psychology with respect to physical sciences, emphasizing the unique epistemological status of psychological knowledge.

   - **Location/Context**: This quotation follows the initial premise, emphasizing the necessity of psycho-physical correlations.
   - **Significance**: It reinforces the idea that psychological truths cannot be deduced from physiological truths without knowledge of correlations, supporting the non-reductive thesis.

   - **Location/Context**: This quotation follows the discussion of correlations, emphasizing the non-deducibility of psychological truths from physiological truths.
   - **Significance**: It directly supports the core thesis that psychological truths are fundamentally different from physiological truths, reinforcing the non-reductive position.

## SECTION 7: CONCEPTUAL APPARATUS

### 7.1 Key Terms and Their Definitions

1. **Psycho-Physical Correlations**: Correlations between microphysical propositions about the brain and psychological truths about a person.

2. **Co-Extensiveness**: The property of two types being co-extensive if all instances of one type are instances of the other type, though not necessarily vice versa.

3. **Knowledge by Acquaintance**: Direct, intuitive knowledge of one's own mental states.

4. **Knowledge by Description**: Second-hand knowledge of others' mental states, informed by non-perceptual knowledge of one's own states.

5. **Non-Perceptual Knowledge**: Knowledge that is not based on sense-perception, particularly relevant to understanding psychological states.

### 7.2 Technical Vocabulary Introduced or Employed

1. **Psychological Types**: Categories of psychological states or abilities.

2. **Physiological Types**: Categories of physiological states or structures.

3. **Migration of Psychological Abilities**: The process by which psychological abilities can be mediated by different neurological structures.

### 7.3 Distinctions Drawn Between Related Concepts

1. **Psychological Truths vs. Physiological Truths**: The text distinguishes between psychological truths, which cannot be deduced from physiological truths, and physiological truths, which are about the physical structure and function of the body.

2. **Co-Extensive Types vs. Necessarily Co-Extensive Types**: The text distinguishes between types that are co-extensive (all instances of one type are instances of the other) and types that are necessarily co-extensive (it is impossible for one type to exist without the other).

3. **Knowledge by Acquaintance vs. Knowledge by Description**: The text distinguishes between direct, intuitive knowledge of one's own mental states and second-hand knowledge of others' mental states.

### 7.4 Theoretical Innovations or Neologisms

1. **Anomaly of Psychology**: The text introduces the concept of the anomaly of psychology with respect to physical sciences, emphasizing the unique epistemological status of psychological knowledge.

### 7.5 Repurposing of Existing Concepts

1. **Functionalism**: The text repurposes the functionalist theory of mind to support its argument about the migration of psychological abilities across different neurological structures.

3. **Epistemological Internalism**: The text's alignment with epistemological internalism could be challenged by externalist views of knowledge, which emphasize external factors in determining knowledge.

2. **Objections from Neuroscience**: The text could engage more directly with objections from neuroscience, which might argue for a more direct reduction of psychological states to physiological states.

3. **Objections from Epistemology**: The text could address objections from epistemologists who might challenge the distinction between knowledge by acquaintance and knowledge by description, or the emphasis on non-perceptual knowledge.

2. **Limitations**: The text's arguments are limited to the context of non-reductive physicalism and functionalism, and do not fully address alternative philosophical positions or empirical evidence.

## SECTION 10: IMPLICATIONS & CONSEQUENCES

### 10.1 Theoretical Implications

1. **Non-Reductive Physicalism**: The text's arguments support non-reductive physicalism, suggesting that psychological states cannot be fully reduced to physiological states.

2. **Functionalism**: The text's discussion of the migration of psychological abilities supports functionalist theories of mind, emphasizing the functional roles of psychological states.

3. **Epistemology of Psychology**: The text's emphasis on non-perceptual knowledge has implications for the epistemology of psychology, suggesting that psychological knowledge is fundamentally different from physical knowledge.

### 10.2 Practical Consequences

1. **Psychological Research**: The text's arguments suggest that psychological research should focus on understanding psychological states in their own terms, rather than attempting to reduce them to physiological explanations.

2. **Clinical Psychology**: The text's emphasis on non-perceptual knowledge has practical implications for clinical psychology, suggesting that understanding patients' mental states requires more than just physiological data.

3. **Interdisciplinary Collaboration**: The text's discussion of the anomaly of psychology with respect to physical sciences suggests that interdisciplinary collaboration between psychology and physical sciences should focus on establishing correlations rather than reductions.

### 10.3 What Follows if the Thesis is Correct

1. **Non-Reducibility of Psychology**: If the thesis is correct, psychology cannot be reduced to physiology, and psychological truths must be understood in their own terms.

2. **Importance of Non-Perceptual Knowledge**: If the thesis is correct, non-perceptual knowledge is crucial for understanding psychological states, both in oneself and in others.

3. **Functionalist Approach**: If the thesis is correct, a functionalist approach to psychological states is supported, allowing for the migration of psychological abilities across different neurological structures.

### 10.4 What's at Stake in Accepting or Rejecting the Position

1. **Philosophical Understanding of Mind**: Accepting the position supports a non-reductive physicalist and functionalist understanding of the mind, while rejecting it might align with reductive physicalism or dualism.

2. **Epistemological Foundations**: Accepting the position emphasizes the importance of non-perceptual knowledge in understanding psychological states, while rejecting it might prioritize sense-based knowledge.

3. **Interdisciplinary Relations**: Accepting the position suggests that psychology and physical sciences should focus on establishing correlations, while rejecting it might support attempts at reduction.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement
1. **Primary Formulation**: The text argues that psychological truths are anomalous with respect to physical truths, meaning that mental states cannot be fully explained or reduced to physical states.
2. **Secondary Formulation**: The text critiques Donald Davidson's attempt to demonstrate the anomalousness of the mental with respect to the physical, arguing that his approach fails due to false assumptions.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis
1. **Argument 1: Non-Reductionist Nature of Psychological Truths**
   - **Explanation**: The text asserts that propositions like "Sam is sad" are not reducible to physical descriptions of mass-energy distributions. This is supported by the claim that such propositions register truths known in a non-perceptual way, which cannot be fully captured by physical descriptions.
2. **Argument 2: Critique of Davidson's Interpretivism**
   - **Explanation**: The text critiques Davidson's argument that mental states are constituted by their interpretability. It argues that this view is false because it implies that people cannot make erroneous inferences or act irrationally, which contradicts observable human behavior.

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims
1. **Subsidiary Claim 1: Mediation by Physiological Events**
   - **Explanation**: The text acknowledges that mental states like sadness may be mediated by physiological events but maintains that this does not entail that mental propositions are about physical states.
2. **Subsidiary Claim 2: Explanatory Gap**
   - **Explanation**: The text agrees with Davidson that there is an explanatory gap between mental and physical states but argues that Davidson's specific argument fails to establish this gap convincingly.

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments
1. **Assumption 1: Non-Reducibility of Mental States**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes that mental states have a non-reducible aspect that cannot be fully captured by physical descriptions.
2. **Assumption 2: Rationality and Interpretability**
   - **Explanation**: The critique of Davidson's interpretivism assumes that human rationality and interpretability are not sufficient to define mental states, as people can act irrationally.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument
1. **Premise 1**: Psychological truths are not reducible to physical truths.
2. **Premise 2**: Davidson's attempt to demonstrate the anomalousness of the mental with respect to the physical relies on false assumptions.
3. **Conclusion**: Therefore, Davidson's argument fails to establish the anomalousness of the mental, but the anomalousness remains a valid concept.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text
1. **Introduction of the Thesis**: The text begins by asserting the non-reducibility of psychological truths to physical truths, using the example of "Sam is sad."
2. **Critique of Davidson's Argument**: The text then shifts to a detailed critique of Davidson's argument, focusing on his interpretivist view of mental states.
3. **Refutation of Interpretivism**: The critique is developed through a logical analysis of Davidson's assumptions, leading to the conclusion that his argument fails.
4. **Reaffirmation of the Thesis**: Despite the critique of Davidson, the text reaffirms the thesis that psychological truths are anomalous with respect to physical truths.

   - **Location/Context**: This quotation elaborates on the nature of the proposition "Sam is sad," emphasizing its non-perceptual and subjective aspects.

8. **Quotation 8**: "Suppose the following. x is some event in Smith's brain. x also constitutes a desire on Smith's part to become a lawyer."
   - **Location/Context**: This is part of Davidson's argument as presented in the text, illustrating his approach to mental states.

9. **Quotation 9**: "If this were right, nobody could possibly make any erroneous inferences; nobody could ever think or act irrationally."
   - **Location/Context**: This appears in the critique of Davidson's interpretivism, highlighting a logical consequence of his view.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

1. **Primary Formulation**: The immediate intention of a scientist is to construct a model that satisfies certain desiderata, rather than to directly describe the truth.
   - **Secondary Formulation**: Scientific explanation involves showing the apparently discontinuous to be continuous within the framework of the data and concepts specific to a discipline.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Argument 1: Models as Intermediaries to Truth**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that since truth is known only through the models scientists construct, the immediate goal of scientific inquiry is to build models that are hoped to be truth-conducive but are primarily evaluated based on other criteria.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text states, "But, since truth is known only through the models we construct, it cannot be a scientist's immediate intention to describe the truth; his immediate intention must be to construct a model."

2. **Argument 2: Data-Internal Continuity as the Goal of Explanation**
   - **Explanation**: The text posits that scientific explanation involves establishing continuity within the data itself, using concepts specific to the discipline, rather than aiming for continuity in a data-transcendent reality.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text explains, "Given this fact, along with the fact that to explain is to show the apparently discontinuous to be continuous, it follows that a scientist's immediate intention to establish data-internal continuity, not among in reality per se..."

3. **Argument 3: Explanatory Robustness of Concepts**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that concepts with explanatory value are those that lead to explanations that are robust and lead to truth, and these concepts are central to the construction of scientific models.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text defines explanatory robustness and states, "A given piece of knowledge is 'explanatorily robust' if the proposition thereby known is explanatorily robust, and a proposition is 'explanatory robust' if it leads to explanations that lead to the truth."

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **Subsidiary Claim 1: Data Differentiation into Sub-Classes**
   - **Explanation**: The text claims that data naturally differentiates into sub-classes based on shared concepts, which aids in the organization and modeling of data.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text notes, "The body of data at a person's disposal isn't homogenous. It automatically differentiates itself into sub-classes of data."

2. **Subsidiary Claim 2: Discipline-Specific Continuities**
   - **Explanation**: The text asserts that scientists seek to establish continuities within their specific discipline, using concepts relevant to that discipline, rather than seeking universal continuities.
   - **Supporting Evidence**: The text uses the example of a botanist, stating, "So a botanist wants to avoid botanical discontinuities."

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Assumption 1: Models as Essential Tools for Scientific Inquiry**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes that models are indispensable for scientific understanding and that truth is mediated through these models.

2. **Assumption 2: The Value of Explanatory Robustness**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes that the explanatory robustness of a concept or proposition is a valuable criterion for scientific inquiry and model construction.

3. **Assumption 3: Discipline-Specific Concepts as Valid for Scientific Explanation**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes that concepts specific to a discipline are sufficient for establishing scientific explanations, even if they do not align with a more universal or microphysical understanding.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

1. **Premise 1**: Truth is known only through models.
2. **Premise 2**: The immediate intention of a scientist is to construct a model.
3. **Premise 3**: Models are evaluated based on criteria independent of truth.
4. **Premise 4**: Scientific explanation involves showing the discontinuous to be continuous.
5. **Premise 5**: Continuity is established within the data using discipline-specific concepts.
6. **Conclusion**: The immediate goal of scientific inquiry is to construct models that establish data-internal continuity, not to directly describe the truth.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

1. **Introduction of Functionalism**: The text begins by referencing a doctrine called "functionalism," which it will later argue against.
2. **Role of Models**: The text then introduces the central role of models in scientific inquiry, arguing that models are the primary focus of scientists.
3. **Data Differentiation**: It discusses how data naturally differentiates into sub-classes, which is crucial for model construction.
4. **Continuity and Explanation**: The text elaborates on the concept of continuity and how it relates to scientific explanation, emphasizing data-internal continuity.
5. **Explanatory Robustness**: The concept of explanatory robustness is introduced and linked to the construction of models and the evaluation of scientific concepts.
6. **Discipline-Specific Examples**: The text uses examples from botany to illustrate its points about discipline-specific continuities and the construction of models.
7. **Conclusion on Data and Concepts**: The text concludes by revisiting the concept of a datum and how it relates to the construction of models and the pursuit of truth.

## SECTION 7: CONCEPTUAL APPARATUS

### 7.1 Key Terms and Their Definitions

1. **Datum**: The text defines a datum as a piece of knowledge that is part of the smallest set containing all of the propositions that one accepts by virtue of having pieces of knowledge.
   - **Definition**: "Consider the smallest set containing all of those beliefs of yours that are cases of explanatorily robust knowledge."

2. **Explanatory Robustness**: The text defines a proposition as 'explanatory robust' if it leads to explanations that lead to the truth.
   - **Definition**: "A given piece of knowledge is 'explanatorily robust' if the proposition thereby known is explanatorily robust, and a proposition is 'explanatory robust' if it leads to explanations that lead to the truth."

3. **Continuity**: The text defines scientific explanation as showing the apparently discontinuous to be continuous.
   - **Definition**: "Given this fact, along with the fact that to explain is to show the apparently discontinuous to be continuous..."

4. **Data-Internal Continuity**: The text defines data-internal continuity as establishing continuity within the data itself, using concepts specific to a discipline.
   - **Definition**: "...it follows that a scientist's immediate intention to establish data-internal continuity, not among in reality per se..."

5. **Data-Transcendent Continuity**: The text defines data-transcendent continuity as establishing continuity in a broader, data-transcendent reality.
   - **Definition**: "...not in the data-transcendent reality to which, if all goes well, his models will lead him..."

### 7.2 Technical Vocabulary Introduced or Employed

1. **Model**: The text uses the term 'model' to refer to the constructs that scientists build to understand and explain data.
   - **Use**: "But, since truth is known only through the models we construct, it cannot be a scientist's immediate intention to describe the truth; his immediate intention must be to construct a model."

2. **Desideratum**: The text uses the term 'desideratum' to refer to the criteria by which models are evaluated, independent of truth.
   - **Use**: "...his immediate intention is to construct a model that, although hoped to be truth-conducive, is intended to satisfy some desideratum that can be identified without already knowing the relevant truth..."

3. **Sub-Class**: The text uses the term 'sub-class' to refer to the natural differentiation of data into categories based on shared concepts.
   - **Use**: "The body of data at a person's disposal isn't homogenous. It automatically differentiates itself into sub-classes of data."

### 7.3 Distinctions Drawn Between Related Concepts

1. **Data-Internal vs. Data-Transcendent Continuity**: The text distinguishes between establishing continuity within the data itself and in a broader, data-transcendent reality.
   - **Distinction**: "...it follows that a scientist's immediate intention to establish data-internal continuity, not among in reality per se..."

2. **Explanatory Robust vs. Non-Robust Concepts**: The text distinguishes between concepts that lead to robust explanations and those that do not.
   - **Distinction**: "A given piece of knowledge is 'explanatorily robust' if the proposition thereby known is explanatorily robust, and a proposition is 'explanatory robust' if it leads to explanations that lead to the truth."

   - **Distinction**: "So a botanist wants to avoid botanical discontinuities... True---propositions describing such botanical continuities are exponentially more low-resolution than those describing microphysical continuities."

2. **Premise 2**: The immediate intention of a scientist is to construct a model.
3. **Premise 3**: Models are evaluated based on criteria independent of truth.
4. **Premise 4**: Scientific explanation involves showing the discontinuous to be continuous.
5. **Premise 5**: Continuity is established within the data using discipline-specific concepts.
6. **Conclusion**: The immediate goal of scientific inquiry is to construct models that establish data-internal continuity, not to directly describe the truth.

### 8.4 Use of Examples, Illustrations, or Case Studies

1. **Botanical Example**: The text uses the example of a botanist to illustrate the concept of discipline-specific continuities and the construction of models.
   - **Use**: "So a botanist wants to avoid botanical discontinuities."

2. **Dogber Thought Experiment**: The text introduces the concept of a 'dogber' as a thought experiment to illustrate the difference between concepts with and without explanatory value.
   - **Use**: "Let a dogber be something that is either a dog or a number."

3. **Plant Concept**: The text uses the concept of a plant to illustrate the explanatory value of certain concepts.
   - **Use**: "The world is so structured that, given that x is a plant, it follows that it will thrive under certain conditions and die under others..."

### 8.5 Anticipation and Response to Objections

1. **Objection to Data-Internal Continuity**: The text anticipates the objection that data-internal continuity might be less accurate than data-transcendent continuity.
   - **Response**: The text acknowledges this objection but argues that data-internal continuity is necessary for scientific explanation within a discipline. "True---propositions describing such botanical continuities are exponentially more low-resolution than those describing microphysical continuities."

2. **Objection to Model Construction as the Immediate Goal**: The text anticipates the objection that the immediate goal of scientific inquiry should be to describe the truth.
   - **Response**: The text argues that since truth is known only through models, the immediate goal must be model construction. "But, since truth is known only through the models we construct, it cannot be a scientist's immediate intention to describe the truth; his immediate intention must be to construct a model."

### 8.6 Dialectical Structure

1. **Engagement with Functionalism**: The text engages dialectically with the doctrine of functionalism, which it will later argue against.
   - **Structure**: This engagement sets the stage for the text's argument against functionalism and in favor of its own position on scientific inquiry.

2. **Engagement with Realism vs. Anti-Realism**: The text engages dialectically with the debate between scientific realism and anti-realism by suggesting that while models aim to lead to truth, the immediate goal is model construction.
   - **Structure**: This engagement helps to clarify the text's position within this ongoing debate.

## SECTION 10: IMPLICATIONS & CONSEQUENCES

### 10.1 Theoretical Implications

1. **Reconceptualization of Scientific Inquiry**: The text's argument that the immediate goal of scientific inquiry is model construction rather than the direct pursuit of truth suggests a reconceptualization of the nature of scientific inquiry.
   - **Implication**: This reconceptualization challenges traditional views of scientific inquiry as a direct pursuit of truth and emphasizes the role of models as intermediaries.

2. **Focus on Data-Internal Continuity**: The text's emphasis on data-internal continuity as the focus of scientific explanation has implications for how scientific explanations are constructed and evaluated.
   - **Implication**: This focus suggests that scientific explanations should be evaluated based on their ability to establish continuity within the data itself, rather than their correspondence to an external reality.

3. **Value of Explanatory Robustness**: The text's introduction of the concept of explanatory robustness as a criterion for evaluating scientific concepts and propositions has implications for the philosophy of science.
   - **Implication**: This concept suggests that scientific concepts and propositions should be evaluated based on their ability to lead to robust explanations, rather than other criteria such as predictive power or simplicity.

### 10.2 Practical Consequences

1. **Scientific Practice**: The text's arguments have practical consequences for how scientists approach their work, emphasizing the construction of models as the immediate goal of scientific inquiry.
   - **Consequence**: Scientists may focus more on developing and refining models that satisfy certain desiderata, rather than directly seeking to describe the truth.

2. **Scientific Education**: The text's emphasis on the role of models and data-internal continuity has implications for scientific education, particularly in how scientific concepts and methods are taught.
   - **Consequence**: Educators may emphasize the importance of model construction and the evaluation of models based on criteria independent of truth.

3. **Interdisciplinary Collaboration**: The text's focus on discipline-specific continuities may have consequences for interdisciplinary collaboration, potentially limiting the integration of different disciplinary perspectives.
   - **Consequence**: Scientists may prioritize discipline-specific explanations over interdisciplinary approaches that seek to integrate different levels of explanation.

### 10.3 What Follows if the Thesis is Correct

1. **Model Construction as the Immediate Goal**: If the text's thesis that the immediate goal of scientific inquiry is model construction is correct, then scientists should focus on developing and refining models that satisfy certain desiderata.
   - **Follows**: This focus would lead to a shift in scientific practice towards model-based reasoning and evaluation.

2. **Data-Internal Continuity as the Focus of Explanation**: If the text's argument that scientific explanation involves establishing data-internal continuity is correct, then scientific explanations should be evaluated based on their ability to establish continuity within the data itself.
   - **Follows**: This focus would lead to a reevaluation of scientific explanations based on their ability to establish data-internal continuity, rather than their correspondence to an external reality.

3. **Explanatory Robustness as a Criterion**: If the text's introduction of explanatory robustness as a criterion for evaluating scientific concepts and propositions is correct, then scientific concepts and propositions should be evaluated based on their ability to lead to robust explanations.
   - **Follows**: This criterion would lead to a shift in how scientific concepts and propositions are evaluated, emphasizing their explanatory power over other criteria.

### 10.4 What's at Stake in Accepting or Rejecting the Position

   - **Stake**: This shift would influence how scientific concepts and propositions are prioritized and developed.
